Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. This is likely due to the ability of these groups to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. The multiple hydroxyl groups can enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of the trifluoromethyl group can destabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

**End of Rules**

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. The multiple hydroxyl groups can enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of the trifluoromethyl group can destabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole structure can enhance π-π interactions and stabilize radical intermediates. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. The multiple hydroxyl groups can enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of the trifluoromethyl group can destabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole structure can enhance π-π interactions and stabilize radical intermediates. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a benzene ring tend to result in a high yield when combined with additional electron-withdrawing groups. This combination can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, promoting radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.714, which is slightly lower than the previous best of 0.8. This suggests that while the rules are capturing some important features, there is room for improvement.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The decrease in validation accuracy from 0.8 to 0.714 suggests potential overfitting to the training data. The model might be capturing noise rather than the underlying patterns, which is not generalizing well to the validation set.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 4 (pyridine ring) and Rule 14 (carboxylic acid on benzene with electron-withdrawing groups) often have high positive SHAP values. This indicates these rules might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 3 (multiple hydroxyl groups) and Rule 11 (benzofuranone group) often have high negative SHAP values, suggesting these rules might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy suggests that the current rules might not be capturing all the necessary features or interactions. The SHAP analysis indicates that some rules might be too strong or too weak, leading to misclassifications.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 4 and Rule 14 are contributing significantly to overestimations, while Rule 3 and Rule 11 are contributing to underestimations. This suggests a need to refine these rules to better capture the true interactions affecting catalyst loading.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 4 (Pyridine Ring):** Consider adding conditions or exceptions where the pyridine ring might not contribute to high loading, such as when combined with certain other functional groups that might deactivate the catalyst.
   - **Rule 14 (Carboxylic Acid on Benzene):** Re-evaluate the electron-withdrawing groups considered in this rule. Some groups might not be as effective in enhancing catalyst performance as initially thought.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 3 (Multiple Hydroxyl Groups):** Investigate the specific configurations or additional functional groups that might enhance the effect of hydroxyl groups, leading to higher loading.
   - **Rule 11 (Benzofuranone Group):** Explore the context in which benzofuranone groups are effective, possibly considering steric effects or additional substituents that might enhance their performance.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more diverse training data to capture a wider range of interactions and reduce overfitting.
   - Consider using regularization techniques to prevent the model from fitting noise in the training data.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively test and refine rules. For example, if a rule consistently shows high SHAP values for incorrect predictions, it should be re-evaluated and adjusted.
   - Continuously monitor validation performance to ensure that changes lead to improvements in generalization and accuracy.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to better capture the true factors influencing catalyst loading, leading to improved model performance and more accurate predictions.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. The multiple hydroxyl groups can enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of the trifluoromethyl group can destabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole structure can enhance π-π interactions and stabilize radical intermediates. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a benzene ring tend to result in a high yield when combined with additional electron-withdrawing groups. This combination can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, promoting radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of the trifluoromethyl group can destabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole structure can enhance π-π interactions and stabilize radical intermediates. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a benzene ring tend to result in a high yield when combined with additional electron-withdrawing groups. This combination can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, promoting radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

**End of Rules**

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. The multiple hydroxyl groups can enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. The multiple hydroxyl groups can enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of the trifluoromethyl group can destabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole structure can enhance π-π interactions and stabilize radical intermediates. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a benzene ring tend to result in a high yield when combined with additional electron-withdrawing groups. This combination can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, promoting radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown an improvement in validation accuracy from previous iterations, with the latest accuracy at 0.7714. This suggests that the rules are becoming more effective in predicting the loading of modifiers on catalysts.
   - The SHAP analysis indicates that certain rules, such as Rule 1 and Rule 2, are consistently contributing positively to high loading predictions, which aligns with the experimental values.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The consistent improvement in validation accuracy suggests that the model is not overfitting. However, the presence of discrepancies where predicted loadings do not match experimental values indicates potential underfitting or areas where the rules could be refined further.

3. **Discrepancy Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 and Rule 4 often have high positive SHAP values. This suggests that these rules might be overestimating the loading in certain cases.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 9 and Rule 10 often have high negative SHAP values, indicating potential underestimation.

4. **Accuracy Improvement Interpretation:**
   - The improvement in accuracy metrics suggests that the current rules are moving in the right direction. However, there is still room for refinement to address the discrepancies noted in the SHAP analysis.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 1 and Rule 2 are generally effective but may need adjustments to avoid overestimation in specific cases.
   - Rules with high negative SHAP values, such as Rule 9, may need to be revisited to ensure they do not lead to underestimation of loading.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 1:** Consider adding conditions or exceptions for specific molecular structures where the rule might not apply, to prevent overestimation.
   - **Rule 4:** Evaluate the specific types of pyridine-containing molecules that might not lead to high loading and adjust the rule accordingly.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 9:** Reassess the impact of trifluoromethyl groups in combination with other functional groups to better capture cases where they might not lead to low yield.
   - **Rule 10:** Investigate the conditions under which indole groups might not enhance yield as expected and refine the rule to account for these exceptions.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more nuanced rules that consider the interaction of multiple functional groups rather than single group effects, to better capture complex molecular interactions.
   - Use cross-validation techniques to ensure that the rules generalize well across different subsets of the data.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Continuously use SHAP analysis to identify rules that consistently contribute to discrepancies and iteratively refine them.
   - Consider incorporating machine learning techniques to dynamically adjust rule weights based on their SHAP values, allowing for more adaptive rule application.

By implementing these recommendations, the rules can be further refined to improve the accuracy and reliability of predictions, ultimately leading to better alignment with experimental results.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability and reactivity. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. The multiple hydroxyl groups can enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield. The strong electron-withdrawing nature of the trifluoromethyl group can destabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole structure can enhance π-π interactions and stabilize radical intermediates. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a benzene ring tend to result in a high yield when combined with additional electron-withdrawing groups. This combination can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, promoting radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons tend to result in a high yield. Longer chains may interfere with coordination. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring without bulky substituents tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a benzene ring tend to result in a high yield when combined with additional electron-withdrawing groups. This combination can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, promoting radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

**End of Rules**

Final Metric Message:

    Target Name: yield
    GPT Model: gpt-4o-2024-08-06

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons tend to result in a high yield. Longer chains may interfere with coordination. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring without bulky substituents tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a benzene ring tend to result in a high yield when combined with additional electron-withdrawing groups. This combination can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, promoting radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown an improvement in validation accuracy, reaching 0.8 in the latest iteration. This suggests that the rules are effective to some extent in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.
   - The consistent validation accuracy across different iterations indicates that the rules are stable and not overly sensitive to changes in the dataset.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The validation accuracy is consistently higher than the test accuracy in previous iterations, which could indicate a slight overfitting to the validation set. However, the current validation accuracy of 0.8 suggests that the model is generalizing better than before.
   - The test accuracy in previous iterations was lower (0.625), indicating potential underfitting or issues with the test set. It would be beneficial to evaluate the test set further to ensure it is representative of the problem space.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 4 and Rule 14 often have high positive SHAP values. This suggests that these rules might be overestimating the loading in certain cases.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 and Rule 3 often have high negative SHAP values, indicating potential underestimation.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The improvement in validation accuracy to 0.8 suggests that the current rules are moving towards a more accurate representation of the data. However, the discrepancies in SHAP values indicate that there is still room for refinement.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 4 and Rule 14 are contributing significantly to high loading predictions. These rules might need refinement to prevent overestimation.
   - Rules with high negative SHAP values, such as Rule 1 and Rule 3, should be adjusted to better capture cases where the experimental loading is high.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 4**: Consider adding conditions to account for bulky substituents or other factors that might reduce the effectiveness of pyridine coordination.
   - **Rule 14**: Evaluate the specific electron-withdrawing groups that are most effective and refine the rule to focus on these groups.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 1**: Explore additional functional groups or structural features that might enhance coordination with metal centers, beyond the current scope.
   - **Rule 3**: Investigate the specific configurations of hydroxyl groups that are most effective in enhancing solubility and interaction.

3. **Generalization Improvements:**
   - Introduce more diverse examples in the training set to ensure the rules are not overly tailored to specific cases.
   - Consider using cross-validation techniques to better assess the generalization capability of the rules.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively test and refine the rules, focusing on the rules with the most significant SHAP values.
   - Continuously evaluate the impact of rule changes on both validation and test accuracy to ensure improvements are consistent across datasets.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve both accuracy and generalization, reducing discrepancies between predicted and experimental values.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can enhance coordination with the metal centers. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure, tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring without bulky substituents tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a benzene ring tend to result in a high yield when combined with additional electron-withdrawing groups. This combination can enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, promoting radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons, especially when the chain is branched, tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can enhance coordination with the metal centers and improve spatial arrangement for better interaction. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure with specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring without bulky substituents or with small electron-withdrawing groups like fluorine tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

**End of Rules**

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons, especially when the chain is branched, tend to result in a high yield. These functional groups can enhance coordination with the metal centers and improve spatial arrangement for better interaction. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure with specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring without bulky substituents or with small electron-withdrawing groups like fluorine tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, potentially stabilizing the active site. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons, especially when the chain is branched or has specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. The spatial arrangement and potential for hydrogen bonding enhance interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups increase the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure with specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional electron-withdrawing groups such as carboxylic acid or halogens tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and the electron-withdrawing groups enhance this interaction. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of an amino group (-NH2) and a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield. This combination can enhance coordination and stabilize the catalyst through multiple interactions. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons, especially when the chain is branched or has specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. The spatial arrangement and potential for hydrogen bonding enhance interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups increase the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure with specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional electron-withdrawing groups such as carboxylic acid or halogens tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and the electron-withdrawing groups enhance this interaction. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of an amino group (-NH2) and a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield. This combination can enhance coordination and stabilize the catalyst through multiple interactions. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons, especially when the chain is branched or has specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. This is due to enhanced hydrogen bonding and spatial arrangement that facilitates interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups increase the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

(e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and the electron-withdrawing groups enhance this interaction. Evaluate the impact of steric hindrance or specific electronic effects from additional substituents. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown an improvement in validation accuracy, reaching 0.8 in several iterations, which is higher than the previous reference accuracies of 0.76. This suggests that the rules are generally effective in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy of 0.8 across multiple iterations suggests that the model is not overfitting to the training data. However, the presence of discrepancies where predicted loadings do not match experimental values indicates potential underfitting or areas where the rules could be refined.

3. **Discrepancy Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 4 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading. Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 and Rule 3 frequently have high negative SHAP values, suggesting they might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The improvement in validation accuracy from 0.76 to 0.8 suggests that the current rules have been refined effectively. However, the presence of discrepancies indicates that further improvements are possible.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 4, which involves pyridine rings with electron-withdrawing groups, might be too aggressive in predicting high loadings. Similarly, Rule 1 and Rule 3, which involve amino and hydroxyl groups, might need adjustments to better capture the conditions under which they lead to high loadings.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Loadings:**
   - **Rule 4:** Consider adding conditions to account for steric hindrance or specific electronic effects that might reduce the effectiveness of pyridine rings with electron-withdrawing groups. This could involve evaluating the impact of additional substituents that might interfere with the interaction with the catalyst.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Loadings:**
   - **Rule 1 and Rule 3:** These rules could be refined by incorporating additional structural features or conditions that enhance the interaction with the catalyst. For example, considering the spatial arrangement or the presence of additional functional groups that might enhance or inhibit the interaction.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more nuanced conditions in the rules to capture a broader range of molecular interactions. This could involve using machine learning techniques to identify patterns in the data that are not currently captured by the rules.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. This could involve testing new hypotheses about molecular interactions and incorporating them into the rules, followed by evaluation against the validation set to assess their effectiveness.

5. **Address Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - Consider using cross-validation techniques to ensure that the rules generalize well to unseen data. This could involve splitting the data into multiple folds and evaluating the rules across these folds to identify any overfitting or underfitting issues.

By implementing these recommendations, the current rules can be further refined to improve their accuracy and generalization, ultimately leading to better predictions of modifier loadings on catalysts.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) and an amino group (-NH2) on an aliphatic chain with less than 6 carbons, especially when the chain is branched or has specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. This is due to enhanced hydrogen bonding and spatial arrangement that facilitates interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, 5-aminopentanoic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro (-NO2) or halogens (e.g., bromine) tend to result in a high yield. These groups increase the electron-deficient nature of the catalyst, facilitating radical formation. (e.g., 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups (-OH) on a carbon chain, especially in a sugar-like structure with specific stereochemistry, tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst. Consider additional functional groups that may enhance these interactions. (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with additional electron-withdrawing groups such as carboxylic acid or halogens tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen in the pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and the electron-withdrawing groups enhance this interaction. Evaluate the impact of steric hindrance or specific electronic effects from additional substituents. (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (-SH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a formyl group (-CHO) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a low yield. The formyl group can potentially deactivate the catalyst by forming stable complexes. (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a methoxy group (-OCH3) on an aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The electron-donating nature of methoxy groups can interfere with the radical mechanism. (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without any polar or reactive groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of functional groups reduces interaction with the catalyst. (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a trifluoromethyl group (-CF3) tend to result in a low yield unless combined with other electron-withdrawing groups that can stabilize the radical intermediates. (e.g., 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield unless the indole is heavily substituted with bulky groups that hinder its stabilizing effect. (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a benzofuranone group tend to result in a high yield. The benzofuranone structure can facilitate electron delocalization, enhancing radical stability. Consider the influence of substituents on the benzofuranone ring. (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a phenyl group substituted with a hydroxyl group (-OH) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of a hydroxyl group on a phenyl ring can lead to unwanted side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid)

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with a methyl group (-CH3) tend to result in a low yield. The methyl group can lead to steric hindrance, reducing the efficiency of the catalyst interaction. (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid)

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a benzene ring substituted with an amino group (-NH2) tend to result in a high yield. The amino group can enhance coordination with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of an amino group (-NH2) and a carboxylic acid group (-COOH) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield. This combination can enhance coordination and stabilize the catalyst through multiple interactions. (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid)
